  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_02f858fe-d9d0-4cb3-9650-6b1209bdf514'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<span id="freeTextContainer4157672354876709047">I won't go into depth as this is a book I've read several times since first encountering it as a teenager ages ago and there are literally hundreds of detailed reviews. One thing that inspires me about Dragonsinger, though, is that it doesn't follow the contemporary form of having everything go wrong for the main character all the way through until the end. In contrast, Menolly actually succeeds in almost every chapter--a victory for her shared with the reader--and yet somehow McCaffrey still ma</span>
  <span id="freeText4157672354876709047" style="display:none">I won't go into depth as this is a book I've read several times since first encountering it as a teenager ages ago and there are literally hundreds of detailed reviews. One thing that inspires me about Dragonsinger, though, is that it doesn't follow the contemporary form of having everything go wrong for the main character all the way through until the end. In contrast, Menolly actually succeeds in almost every chapter--a victory for her shared with the reader--and yet somehow McCaffrey still made the drama feel genuine throughout.</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7743282911003863644">My favorite of the Harper Hall books! There's something so satisfying about Menolly, who went through hell in the first book, being treated like a princess, and shown around the Harper Hall. Getting new clothes, meeting new friends, finding out that her scarred hand will heal . . . just so satisfying!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_30573773" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30573773" />
    <a title="Janni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1292720-janni"><img alt="Janni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553197686p2/1292720.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30573773?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janni" href="/user/show/1292720-janni">Janni</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1292720-janni?shelf=comfort-reads">comfort-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30573773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12411224807379856102">Dragonsinger is very much one of those books that, while on the surface about music--Menolly, the protagonist, is a young musician who's just gotten the chance to train professionally, essentially, after earlier being told she had no right to play at all--is really about pursuing any art.<br/><br/>Dragonsinger falls a bit more strongly toward believing that artists are somehow a little different than others, something I'm not convinced of. But mostly it gets things right, and there's one thing I noticed t</span>
  <span id="freeText12411224807379856102" style="display:none">Dragonsinger is very much one of those books that, while on the surface about music--Menolly, the protagonist, is a young musician who's just gotten the chance to train professionally, essentially, after earlier being told she had no right to play at all--is really about pursuing any art.<br /><br />Dragonsinger falls a bit more strongly toward believing that artists are somehow a little different than others, something I'm not convinced of. But mostly it gets things right, and there's one thing I noticed this time that I hadn't really before: Menolly is a bit self-depreciating about her music at first, because she's been told for so long it wasn't worth much. So when she also claims her music isn't very good, and keeps pointing to all the things she needs to learn--in earlier readings I thought it unwarranted modesty, a lack of acceptance of her own skill.<br /><br />But this reading, I think I get it more: Menolly isn't modest because she fails to accept her own skill. She's modest precisely because of that skill--she's so good, she can see all she has to learn, while a lesser musician, who couldn't see all Menolly can, would be more likely to accept his playing as pretty good. Menolly's modesty isn't a denial of her skill; it's a direct consequence of it.<br /><br />I find myself pondering now whether believing without reservation that you're good at a craft is beginner territory, and whether doubts--or at least a sense of still having a lot to learn--is a sign of having gained some amount of skill, rather than of lacking it.<br /><br />Dragonsinger is also very much about how even when you have things to learn, though, that doesn't mean your work lacks merit, or can't be enjoyed even in its imperfect state--or that those imperfections will always even get in the way of what the work is trying to do. Which is something Menolly does still have to learn: that her work may not be perfect, that she may have things to learn, but that doesn't mean her current creations are unworthy of being loved by others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12411224807379856102" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_793623393" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/793623393" />
    <a title="Harold Ogle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/198484-harold-ogle"><img alt="Harold Ogle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317340769p2/198484.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/793623393?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harold Ogle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harold Ogle" href="/user/show/198484-harold-ogle">Harold Ogle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=dragons">dragons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=mv-library">mv-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/198484-harold-ogle?shelf=bard">bard</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer793623393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15399724621435359407">I think it’s hard for modern readers to imagine the cultural landscape back before fantasy became commonplace and widely accepted. Back when McCaffrey wrote this, before <i>Star Wars</i>, there was very little presence of fantasy in pop culture. Sure, there was plenty of good fantasy to read if you knew where to look, but for most of the general populace, awareness of fantasy was limited to <u>The Lord of the Rings</u>, which had been published twenty years earlier. So when McCaffrey published her Pern novels</span>
  <span id="freeText15399724621435359407" style="display:none">I think it’s hard for modern readers to imagine the cultural landscape back before fantasy became commonplace and widely accepted. Back when McCaffrey wrote this, before <i>Star Wars</i>, there was very little presence of fantasy in pop culture. Sure, there was plenty of good fantasy to read if you knew where to look, but for most of the general populace, awareness of fantasy was limited to <u>The Lord of the Rings</u>, which had been published twenty years earlier. So when McCaffrey published her Pern novels, <b>they took the world by storm</b>. Each book lived on the bestseller lists; every home had a copy; the Pern novels were used to launch the kind of marketing juggernaut that’s expected today but which was astonishing at the time. Everywhere I went, growing up, Pern and Lord of the Rings merchandise abounded. Calendars, atlases, video games, lunch boxes, T-shirts, posters, costumes, toys, stuffed animals: there seemed no amount of the stuff that could satisfy the public appetite for Pern materials. I have to admit, I was turned off and intimidated by all the hype, and so it was this long before I got around to reading them.<br /><br />I’m glad I did, at last. It is a good read, even if the break between the first and second books seems arbitrary. I’ve said many times in the past that the best way to appreciate books in a series is to space them out with plenty of time between one book/episode/installment/issue and the next. Reading series installments back-to-back can often lead to dissatisfaction with voice, style, and even thematic changes, as the author has changed in the time it took between writing different volumes. That said, it’s always interesting to read a book that begins immediately where the last book left off, without any break at all. I’m left wondering whether the books were actually written as a single volume that was then split into parts by the publisher, as with <u>Hyperion</u> and <u>The Lord of the Rings</u>. So it is here: <u>Dragonsinger</u> picks up the narrative of <u>Dragonsong</u> without any pause more than a paragraph break. I actually found this a bit refreshing, as it also means that the book makes no attempt to present “our story so far:” if you haven’t read <u>Dragonsong</u> before this, you’re out of luck. There are no explanations of setting, background, personality or appearance of characters from the first book, or anything else. I loved that.<br /><br />The break between novels does make some sense, if you can accept the premise that a break was needed at all. Our heroine, Menolly, was isolated and on her own in the first book. It was almost a <u>Robinson Crusoe</u> story of survival against the elements, ending with her joyous acceptance back into society. This book is about <u>school</u>, and as such it somewhat feels like a precursor to Harry Potter and all its imitators, if they were set in a music college in a medieval setting...essentially a school for bards. While there is no prophecy presaging Menolly’s arrival (hallelujah!), she is possessed of preternatural gifts: both an amazing musician and a tamer of fire lizards. But this is not a story about magic: it’s a story about Menolly coming to accept her gifts despite the intense prejudice and criticism she has experienced for those gifts up until this point. She is exceptionally talented as a musician, lyricist, and composer, but it takes quite some time for her to first recognize that these things are important and praiseworthy. That’s really what the story arc in the book is: it’s very internal, and not a lot happens externally in the book, which takes place over the course of one week (though I did cheer when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Menolly hauled off and clocked the Lord Holder’s son after everyone was a jerk to her <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>). She moves from self-doubt to self-confidence, makes some enemies and makes some friends. That’s about it, though there were some events (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the dragonrider warping to <i>another planet</i> to try to destroy the source of the Thread! Good grief! What an idiot! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) that I assume foreshadow future developments. It’ll seem sparse and the main character may even seem obtuse to fans of heroic fantasy (“Why doesn’t she get how great she is??”), but I enjoyed it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15399724621435359407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2038724131" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2038724131" />
    <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius"><img alt="Deborah Ideiosepius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425943228p2/4802663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2038724131?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Ideiosepius" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius">Deborah Ideiosepius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/954391.Dragonsinger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=2017-reading-challenge">2017-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2038724131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7462784823657957846">This is a continuation of the previous book Drangonsong, and on this re-read I read them sequentially, though when I first discovered these books I did not have that luxury. Though it helps to have read Drangonsong first, McCaffrey comes from the generation of authors where you did NOT publish a book that could not stand on its own (a talent that as a society, we seem to have, MOST regrettably, lost), so it is quite possible to read this book as a standalone.<br/><br/>Menolly has been brought to the Harpe</span>
  <span id="freeText7462784823657957846" style="display:none">This is a continuation of the previous book Drangonsong, and on this re-read I read them sequentially, though when I first discovered these books I did not have that luxury. Though it helps to have read Drangonsong first, McCaffrey comes from the generation of authors where you did NOT publish a book that could not stand on its own (a talent that as a society, we seem to have, MOST regrettably, lost), so it is quite possible to read this book as a standalone.<br /><br />Menolly has been brought to the Harper Hold, her ability with her nine fire-lizards brought her to the attention of The Master Harper and then he discovered she was the 'lost' apprentice from her hold, who had written those amazing songs that everyone wants more of.<br /><br />The child prodigy is an eternal prototype in fiction, here McCaffrey shows everyone how to do it best: Menolly has to overcome her background to allow herself to mature, fit in, learn and take her proper place in the Harper Hold. I found it delightful reading, even better re-reading and in many ways a better storyline than the book which immediately precedes it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7462784823657957846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2329597815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2329597815" />
    <a title="Jerry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5855981-jerry"><img alt="Jerry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520438201p2/5855981.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780689860249" href="/book/show/1120864.Dragonsinger">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Misogynists.</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78469187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10130453115592744407">Gross. I would strongly discourage my daughters from reading this because it encourages a certain kind of female I can't stand: the girl who doesn't get along with other girls (to whom I always want to say "The other girls don't hate you because you're 'special', they hate you because you're insufferable.") The tolerable, even interesting Menolly from the first book (which I gave 4 stars, by the way) disappears into a freaking Mary-Sue, oh so talented, yet so modest, championed by the "good" guy</span>
  <span id="freeText10130453115592744407" style="display:none">Gross. I would strongly discourage my daughters from reading this because it encourages a certain kind of female I can't stand: the girl who doesn't get along with other girls (to whom I always want to say "The other girls don't hate you because you're 'special', they hate you because you're insufferable.") The tolerable, even interesting Menolly from the first book (which I gave 4 stars, by the way) disappears into a freaking Mary-Sue, oh so talented, yet so modest, championed by the "good" guys but loathed by the "bad" guys. Blanket dismissals of the other girls abound, and the only ones she gets along with are the ones who are useful to her, either through being in charge of the "women's" work, or in helping her take down her female rivals. Not even these allies are spared: when she finally claims one of the girls her age as "one of her best friends", Menolly finds her tedious because she isn't as good a musician as Menolly. From being persecuted and determined in the first book, she just becomes spoiled and annoying in this one. Making journeyman as quickly as she does also defies belief, as does her multitude of skills mastered by the age of 15.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10130453115592744407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_396798858" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396798858" />
    <a title="Simon Mcleish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish"><img alt="Simon Mcleish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347099463p2/7915393.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/396798858?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Mcleish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Mcleish" href="/user/show/7915393-simon-mcleish">Simon Mcleish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915393-simon-mcleish?shelf=owned">owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer396798858" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer867131568604839218">Originally published on my blog <a href='http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/2000/09/anne-mccaffrey-dragonsinger-harper-of.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>here</a> in September 2000.<br/><br/>Although, one of the lightest novels in the Pern series, Dragonsinger is one of my favourites. I find it very evocative of what it feels like to take pleasure in making music. McCaffery is of course musical (she was an opera producer before taking up writing), and music plays an important part in a fair number of her novels (the Crystal Singer series and The Ship Who Sang as well as several of the Pern series).<br/><br/>Dragonsinger follows on immedia</span>
  <span id="freeText867131568604839218" style="display:none">Originally published on my blog <a href="http://simonsbookblog.blogspot.co.uk/2000/09/anne-mccaffrey-dragonsinger-harper-of.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a> in September 2000.<br /><br />Although, one of the lightest novels in the Pern series, Dragonsinger is one of my favourites. I find it very evocative of what it feels like to take pleasure in making music. McCaffery is of course musical (she was an opera producer before taking up writing), and music plays an important part in a fair number of her novels (the Crystal Singer series and The Ship Who Sang as well as several of the Pern series).<br /><br />Dragonsinger follows on immediately from Dragonsong, which tells of the early history of Menolly, whose musical nature is despised as impractical by her family. She finally arrives at Harper Craft Hall at the start of this novel, and the story is about how she finds her feet in her new environment, amazed to be somewhere where her gift is not just accepted but encouraged. She is talented even by the standards of the craft, as many people who have had to overcome grave disadvantages in their backgrounds to do what they really want to tend to be. This helps her form relationships with some people, but brings jealous resentment from others.<br /><br />The secret of the way in which Anne McCaffrey writes about music is that she doesn't try too hard. Music is extremely difficult to describe in words, and the experience of music making even more so. Rather than resorting to metaphor or relying on musical knowledge in the reader, McCaffrey concentrates on the emotional content of the music. This is most easily seen in the scene in which Menolly plays in a chamber group for the first time. The impression given to the reader is the based on how Menolly gets caught up in the music, exhilarated by the experience, in the way in which the various parts fit together intricately, and how time means nothing - a lengthy rehearsal seems really brief.<br /><br />The character of Menolly is not without literary faults. She is superhumanly gifted - as a composer of songs (both words and music), as a performer on many instruments that she never seems to practise of have had the opportunity to learn, as an instrument maker. She is too good to be possible. Dragonsinger is not, of course, intended to be a major work of literature, and it succeeds admirably on its own level.</span>
  <a data-text-id="867131568604839218" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_149502247" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="The FountainPenDiva, Old school geek chick and lover of teddy bears" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears"><img alt="The FountainPenDiva, Old school geek chick and lover of teddy bears" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584827425p2/2142927.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149502247?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The FountainPenDiva, Old school geek chick and lover of teddy bears" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The FountainPenDiva, Old school geek chick and lover of teddy bears" href="/user/show/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears">The FountainPenDiva, Old school geek chick and lover of teddy bears</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553225563" href="/book/show/28549.Dragonsinger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=omg-adorable-comfort-books">omg-adorable-comfort-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=re-reading">re-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=characters-musicians">characters-musicians</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=characters-kick-ass-heroines">characters-kick-ass-heroines</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=genre-fantasy">genre-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=books-paperback">books-paperback</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=characters-dragons">characters-dragons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=heroines-who-rock">heroines-who-rock</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2142927-the-fountainpendiva-old-school-geek-chick-and-lover-of-teddy-bears?shelf=books-that-are-keepers">books-that-are-keepers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149502247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9458011410079766869">In <u>Dragonsigner</u> Menolly finds herself at the Harper Hall. Beyond her wildest dreams and all the obstacles placed by her family, she's ready to a new new life doing what she loves - making music. Unfortunately, life in the Hall isn't all she'd hoped. Her possession of nine fire lizards doesn't make her popular with some, and like most novels of this type, Menolly finds herself dealing with the "mean girl" contingent. She has instructors who don't think it's worth their time to train "a girl", esp</span>
  <span id="freeText9458011410079766869" style="display:none">In <u>Dragonsigner</u> Menolly finds herself at the Harper Hall. Beyond her wildest dreams and all the obstacles placed by her family, she's ready to a new new life doing what she loves - making music. Unfortunately, life in the Hall isn't all she'd hoped. Her possession of nine fire lizards doesn't make her popular with some, and like most novels of this type, Menolly finds herself dealing with the "mean girl" contingent. She has instructors who don't think it's worth their time to train "a girl", especially when said girl actually knows a lot, thanks to training by Petiron, Half Circle Sea Hold's elderly Harper (and Robinton's father) and is actually talented. She makes friends with the irrepressible Piemur and begins a lifelong platonic love with Masterharper Robinton. As she slowly grows into her craft, Menolly becomes aware that her very presence is a part of the changing of attitudes on Pern.<br /><br />Given time and age, I would have loved for Menolly to have Menolly have more close female friends her age, though given her past it makes sense that she doesnt. Sure there's Mirrim (who really really needs her own series) and Audiva, one of the girls who stays in the cot. Yes, Menolly is incredibly talented, and she doesn't realize the extent of her talent, but I never thought she crossed into "Mary Sue" territory. However, so what? Fantasy is full of male Gary Stus who've been given a pass just because they're the heroes. I'm perfectly fine with heroines who are GOOD at what they do and aren't willing to dumb themselves down or hide their gifts. <br /><br />And yes, the fire lizards. I still want a fire lizard. Preferably a bronze or a blue.<br /><br />I also have a soft spot for Masterharper Robinton. Fight me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9458011410079766869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2680710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2282152449151595299">This is one of those books that would have been too short practically no matter how long it was. One of my very favourite books, and one of my introductions to the fantasy genre. I love the atmosphere it describes, and would love to experience some lessons at Harper Hall myself... even though I'd probably be more likely to be one of the clueless girls (although not as cruel I hope) than Menolly.<br/><br/>I love the lyrics that start off each chapter. Usually when books have lyrics or poems as introduction</span>
  <span id="freeText2282152449151595299" style="display:none">This is one of those books that would have been too short practically no matter how long it was. One of my very favourite books, and one of my introductions to the fantasy genre. I love the atmosphere it describes, and would love to experience some lessons at Harper Hall myself... even though I'd probably be more likely to be one of the clueless girls (although not as cruel I hope) than Menolly.<br /><br />I love the lyrics that start off each chapter. Usually when books have lyrics or poems as introductions to chapters I just skip them, but here they seem an integral part of the book :)<br /><br />It's a shame no more books have been written about Menolly's life at Harper Hall. I was so disappointed with Dragondrums, because I wanted to read more about Menolly - not Piemur.<br /><br />Love this book - just wish it was longer.<br /><br />Reread in 2016: No matter how often I read this book, it's just not long enough!</span>

<div id="review_2395138668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2395138668" />
    <a title="prcardi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43250213-prcardi"><img alt="prcardi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471708128p2/43250213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2395138668?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="prcardi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="prcardi" href="/user/show/43250213-prcardi">prcardi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553258547" href="/book/show/797088.Dragonsinger">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=medieval-fantasy">medieval-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=pg">pg</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=other-world">other-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=bildungsroman">bildungsroman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43250213-prcardi?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2395138668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15818368221153511353">Storyline: 2/5<br/>Characters: 4/5<br/>Writing Style: 4/5<br/>World: 3/5<br/><br/>I had made up my mind to dislike this before I started. I don't particularly enjoy children's or young adult books, and I didn't approve of the adolescent turn with the Harper Hall series. While Dragonsong was a perfectly acceptable children's version of the Dragonriders of Pern books, it was a simple recycling of the adult story for younger audiences. McCaffrey didn't make that mistake here. Essentially this is a coming-of-age, boarding s</span>
  <span id="freeText15818368221153511353" style="display:none">Storyline: 2/5<br />Characters: 4/5<br />Writing Style: 4/5<br />World: 3/5<br /><br />I had made up my mind to dislike this before I started. I don't particularly enjoy children's or young adult books, and I didn't approve of the adolescent turn with the Harper Hall series. While Dragonsong was a perfectly acceptable children's version of the Dragonriders of Pern books, it was a simple recycling of the adult story for younger audiences. McCaffrey didn't make that mistake here. Essentially this is a coming-of-age, boarding school book set in Pern. We get to follow up with the main character from the first in this series, picking up right where we left off in Dragonsong. <br /><br />It is the characters that make this book so endearing and so difficult to dislike. There is a suffusion of warmth from the Harper characters. McCaffrey takes us along to Harper Hall with a 15-year old's viewpoint and all the anxieties, self-doubt, and worldviews that would have come with one with such a sheltered childhood. We (and by we I mean the reader through the main character of Menolly) meet and interact with the adults with the fear, awe, and uncertainty that would have marked such meetings. And what McCaffrey does so well is to slowly blur that line between adult and child. In my own life I find it takes a steady application of pressure to treat children as such. My inclination is to treat others as my equal, and when a child turns into an adolescent and approaches the young adult age it is difficult to judge when best to let up on that pressure and welcome the teenager into adulthood. McCaffrey offers the reader 15-year old girl's view of how adults negotiate this transition; and this was a treasure.<br /><br />I was also impressed with this as a work intended to empower women. I often find supposedly feminist texts to bring women down to the worst levels of men in order to promote equality. McCaffrey gives us a teenage girl who gets to see traditional gender roles in a wide range - strong, feminine women; sensitive, mischievous boys; dainty, timid girls; boorish, dimwitted boys; and hyper-masculine, male heroes. A mother figure supports Menolly in maintaining her own tomboyish identity while encouraging her to understand and embrace her femininity. A sage-like male figure tutors our adolescent heroine to tolerance, advising her that the old fogeys who would obstruct her because of her gender are people themselves, with more to their person than their sexist ways. What I approve of so whole-heartedly in this book was the message of empowerment and tolerance that refused to steer into radicalism and liberal bigotry. <br /><br />The life of artists and the community of the harpers was another real joy to experience in the book. McCaffrey again showed us the adolescent's worldview as the main character learns that all that seems wondrous is usually backed by the mundane. Artists live in the world, not apart from it, and from washing clothes to cooking or eating food, the life of the artist is more than performance. This even-handedness of McCaffrey's wasn't quite so even with some of the characterization. The good characters tend to be wholly good and the bad characters despicable. Also, while it was a joy to follow a character that was so earnest and self-effacing, the lack of pretensions and ambitions made the tale read as if aimless. There's nothing particular I wanted to happen for Menolly, save emotional approval and friendship. There's no overarching suspense of her achieving any of her goals because they're so modest. Thus the tale was a treasure for its warmth but did little to instill in the reader any aspirations or fears. <br /><br />I've long wanted to find a coming-of-age fantasy work that empowered girls in a positive and wholesome fashion. This was that book. It is unfortunate, however, that it is the 4th book in the overarching Pern series and the 2nd in the Harper Hall series. Thus it is not a book one can just drop into for a singular read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15818368221153511353" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer13286329962900068371">A delightful continuation of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/797079.Dragonsong__Pern_Harper_Hall__1_' title='Dragonsong (Pern Harper Hall #1) by Anne McCaffrey' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dragonsong</a>, here we find Menolly established at the Harper Hall in Fort Hold. She makes friends, and enemies, and learns that her talents and hard work are valued by those around her. After all her tribulations in the first book it's good to see her so happily settled here. <br/><br/>I do think it lacks a bit in plot, and there are some loose ends I'd have preferred to see tied up, so it's not a 5 star book for me. But it is a lovely read that I enjoyed when I was young and ha</span>
  <span id="freeText13286329962900068371" style="display:none">A delightful continuation of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/797079.Dragonsong__Pern_Harper_Hall__1_" title="Dragonsong (Pern Harper Hall #1) by Anne McCaffrey" rel="nofollow noopener">Dragonsong</a>, here we find Menolly established at the Harper Hall in Fort Hold. She makes friends, and enemies, and learns that her talents and hard work are valued by those around her. After all her tribulations in the first book it's good to see her so happily settled here. <br /><br />I do think it lacks a bit in plot, and there are some loose ends I'd have preferred to see tied up, so it's not a 5 star book for me. But it is a lovely read that I enjoyed when I was young and had a great time revisiting. And again I'll repeat it's pity that these books aren't more available at libraries for young readers to discover. </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3534959072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12495057132641925485">A nice story. In a way it reminded me of Harry Potter. This is also a school story, but not a magic school. This novel is about a music school in Anne McCaffrey’s fantasy world of Pern. <br/>The narrative is mediocre, and the stakes are low. No saving the world in this book, just one girl with the gift for music trying to find a place for herself. Of all the books by this author I ever tried to read, the only ones that worked for me so far are <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28541.Dragonsong__Harper_Hall___1_' title='Dragonsong (Harper Hall, #1) by Anne McCaffrey' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dragonsong</a> – Menolly’s first story – and this one – Menol</span>
  <span id="freeText12495057132641925485" style="display:none">A nice story. In a way it reminded me of Harry Potter. This is also a school story, but not a magic school. This novel is about a music school in Anne McCaffrey’s fantasy world of Pern. <br />The narrative is mediocre, and the stakes are low. No saving the world in this book, just one girl with the gift for music trying to find a place for herself. Of all the books by this author I ever tried to read, the only ones that worked for me so far are <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28541.Dragonsong__Harper_Hall___1_" title="Dragonsong (Harper Hall, #1) by Anne McCaffrey" rel="nofollow noopener">Dragonsong</a> – Menolly’s first story – and this one – Menolly’s second story. I liked them both because I liked Menolly. She is a charming protagonist, a young female musician in a world where music is supposed to be men’s business. No woman had even been a harper of Pern. But Menolly’s gift demands music, and she follows her heart while navigating the unfamiliar corridors of a music school with its inevitable factions, petty jealousies, and unexpected friends. <br />Overall: I enjoyed this quiet novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12495057132641925485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText15558331431282989634" style="display:none">Menolly is now in the Harper Hall and slowly coming to realize what he "tunings and twiddlings" can really mean to a people in near constant peril. I do get it. Literacy is rare, books and paper rarer still. They're truly an agrarian society a space faring people fallen almost out of existence. The Harpers and their songs not only teach and entertain but they're also the news and information services. And there's something going on behind the scenes. Harpers doing things unexpected. Turning up and learning, nudging, expanding. This is YA literature at its best. As good as any of Heinlein's juveniles from decades before. </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5390605423690128599">Haha, this is so very much a feel good book. Menolly never really struggles with anything, and her troubles are rather shallow compared to all of her advantages, but I can't help but like the book anyway. <br/><br/>Menolly is finally in Harper Hall and she is training to be a Harper. It turns out (well, we knew this already to a degree) that she's a prodigy and can play instruments to a high degree, make them, and compose songs. Of course this gains her a lot of jealousy, though only really on behalf of a</span>
  <span id="freeText5390605423690128599" style="display:none">Haha, this is so very much a feel good book. Menolly never really struggles with anything, and her troubles are rather shallow compared to all of her advantages, but I can't help but like the book anyway. <br /><br />Menolly is finally in Harper Hall and she is training to be a Harper. It turns out (well, we knew this already to a degree) that she's a prodigy and can play instruments to a high degree, make them, and compose songs. Of course this gains her a lot of jealousy, though only really on behalf of a group of girls who aren't really there to be Harpers, but rather learn at Harper Hall as some sort of prestige thing (kind of like the whole fostering thing the various places do). This made me slightly tired, especially when there's one girl who Menolly gets along with--pretty much the token best girl friend. She gets along well enough with the boys and as well with the Masterharper and several others in high offices. <br /><br />She doesn't exactly put down her talent, but she always finds things wrong with what she does, when everyone else is full of praise. Eh, I get it, after all, it's not <i>perfect</i>, but she doesn't seem to realize that her talent is prodigious. Which, quite frankly, is a lot better than a main character who flaunts their talent everywhere, so I didn't really mind this.<br /><br />There isn't much that happens. It's more of a view into Menolly's new life than any soaring plot, which is fine for me, since I like those kinds of books once in a while. <br /><br />I feel like I keep saying "I don't really mind--", heh. But, okay, it's a very light read, there's very little plot, the main character is pretty much a Mary Sue, the only 'antagonists' are a group of girls who are jealous of Menolly, <i>but I like it anyway.</i> Menolly is a rather charming main character and it was fun reading about this brief stage in her life. 4/5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5390605423690128599" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_634491347" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/634491347" />
    <a title="Adi Greif" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12010-adi-greif"><img alt="Adi Greif" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279672339p2/12010.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/634491347?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adi Greif" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adi Greif" href="/user/show/12010-adi-greif">Adi Greif</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer634491347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11571149042421423379">(See review for Dragonsong). Again, I loved this series and this book as a child. But upon rereading, I find it slow and unclear in its moral message. The main character is an unrealistic mix of traits -- she was essentially emotionally abused as a child and thus is generally overly apologetic and scared of not being liked. Most of the adults constantly praise her humbleness and meek attitude while simultaneously telling her to stop apologizing and believe in herself. Despite her meekness, she o</span>
  <span id="freeText11571149042421423379" style="display:none">(See review for Dragonsong). Again, I loved this series and this book as a child. But upon rereading, I find it slow and unclear in its moral message. The main character is an unrealistic mix of traits -- she was essentially emotionally abused as a child and thus is generally overly apologetic and scared of not being liked. Most of the adults constantly praise her humbleness and meek attitude while simultaneously telling her to stop apologizing and believe in herself. Despite her meekness, she often --seemingly effortlessly-- stands up for herself in brash ways, makes no effort to befriend those who dislike her, and takes charge in emergencies. Then she worries that she overstepped her stations afterward. <br />In addition, almost all of the young women except the main character are portrayed negatively, without any indication that anyone understands the way the school system encourages their negative behavior. The young women just want to gossip about social rank and hot guys and don't care about school. As a child, I sympathized with the main character for disliking them and being teased by them. However, it's clear that the school offers no possible job opportunities above the level of 'cook' for the girls. Why should they practice singing in school when they grew up learning only boys were allowed to get jobs as singers? They are excluded by the teachers from the jobs they are supposed to spend time learning, and then the teachers look down on them for not studying. Hmmm. And why shouldn't they focus on boys, when their marriage will impact their lives far, far more than what they learn in the school? I have to assume the author understood the situation she created. I just wish that one of the more enlightened adults even hinted that they understood it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11571149042421423379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26.Anne_McCaffrey">About Anne McCaffrey</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26.Anne_McCaffrey"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1599324585p3/26.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26.Anne_McCaffrey">Anne McCaffrey
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10585006088781990553">Anne McCaffrey was born on April 1st, 1926, in Cambridge, Massachusetts.<br/><br/>Her parents were George Herbert McCaffrey, BA, MA PhD (Harvard), Colonel USA Army (retired), and Anne Dorothy McElroy McCaffrey, estate agent. She had two brothers: Hugh McCaffrey (deceased 1988), Major US Army, and Kevin Richard McCaffrey, still living.<br/><br/>Anne was educated at Stuart Hall in Staunton Virginia, Montclair High Scho</span>
  <span id="freeText10585006088781990553" style="display:none">Anne McCaffrey was born on April 1st, 1926, in Cambridge, Massachusetts.<br /><br />Her parents were George Herbert McCaffrey, BA, MA PhD (Harvard), Colonel USA Army (retired), and Anne Dorothy McElroy McCaffrey, estate agent. She had two brothers: Hugh McCaffrey (deceased 1988), Major US Army, and Kevin Richard McCaffrey, still living.<br /><br />Anne was educated at Stuart Hall in Staunton Virginia, Montclair High School in Montclair, New Jersey, and graduated cum laude from Radcliffe College, majoring in Slavonic Languages and Literatures.<br /><br />Her working career included Liberty Music Shops and Helena Rubinstein (1947-1952). She married in 1950 and had three children: Alec Anthony, b. 1952, Todd, b.1956, and Georgeanne, b.1959.<br /><br />Anne McCaffrey’s first story was published by Sam Moskowitz in Science Fiction + Magazine and her first novel was published by Ballantine Books in 1967. By the time the three children of her marriage were comfortably in school most of the day, she had already achieved enough success with short stories to devote full time to writing. Her first novel, Restoree, was written as a protest against the absurd and unrealistic portrayals of women in s-f novels in the 50s and early 60s. It is, however, in the handling of broader themes and the worlds of her imagination, particularly the two series The Ship Who Sang and the fourteen novels about the Dragonriders of Pern that Ms. McCaffrey’s talents as a story-teller are best displayed.<br /><br />She died at the age of 85, after suffering a massive stroke on 21 November 2011.<br /><br />Obituaries: <a href="http://www.locusmag.com/News/2011/11/anne-mccaffrey-1926-2011/" rel="nofollow noopener">Locus</a>, <a href="http://www.mediabistro.com/galleycat/anne-mccaffrey-has-died_b42826" rel="nofollow noopener">GalleyCat</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10585006088781990553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/2971170-dragonsinger">Quotes from Dragonsinger</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The tears I feel today<br />I'll wait to shed tomorrow.<br />Though I'll not sleep this night<br />Nor find surcease from sorrow.<br />My eyes must keep their sight:<br />I dare not be tear-blinded.<br />I must be free to talk<br />Not choked with grief, clear-minded.<br />My mouth cannot betray<br />The anguish that I know.<br />Yes, I'll keep my tears til later:<br />But my grief will never go.&rdquo;</span>
